Montvilliers">Matilda FitzRoy, Abbess of Montvilliers</a></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">House</a></th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/House_of_Normandy" title="House of Normandy">Normandy</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Father</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/William_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of England">William I of England</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Mother</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Matilda_of_Flanders" title="Matilda of Flanders">Matilda of Flanders</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Henry I</b> (c. 1068&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 1 December 1135), also known as <b>Henry Beauclerc</b>, was <a href="/wiki/King_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="King of England">King of England</a> from 1100 to his death in 1135. Henry was the fourth son of <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a> and was educated in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_arts" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal arts">liberal arts</a>. On William\'s death in 1087, Henry\'s elder brothers <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curthose" title="Robert Curthose">Robert Curthose</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Rufus" class="mw-redirect" title="William Rufus">William Rufus</a> inherited <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Normandy" title="Duchy of Normandy">Normandy</a> and England, respectively, but Henry was left landless. Henry purchased the <a href="/wiki/Cotentin" class="mw-redirect" title="Cotentin">County of Cotentin</a> in western Normandy from Robert, but William and Robert deposed him in 1091. Henry gradually rebuilt his power base in the Cotentin and allied himself with William against Robert. Henry was present when William died in a hunting accident in 1100, and he seized the English throne, promising at his coronation to correct many of William\'s less popular policies. Henry married <a href="/wiki/Matilda_of_Scotland" title="Matilda of Scotland">Matilda of Scotland</a> but continued to have a large number of mistresses by whom he had many illegitimate children.\n</p><p>Robert, who invaded in 1101, disputed Henry\'s control of England; this military campaign ended in a negotiated settlement that confirmed Henry as king. The peace was short-lived, and Henry invaded the Duchy of Normandy in 1105 and 1106, finally defeating Robert at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tinchebray" title="Battle of Tinchebray">Battle of Tinchebray</a>. Henry kept Robert imprisoned for the rest of his life. Henry\'s control of Normandy was challenged by <a href="/wiki/Louis_VI_of_France" title="Louis VI of France">Louis VI of France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_VII_of_Flanders" class="mw-redirect" title="Baldwin VII of Flanders">Baldwin VII of Flanders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fulk_V_of_Anjou" class="mw-redirect" title="Fulk V of Anjou">Fulk V of Anjou</a>, who promoted the rival claims of Robert\'s son, <a href="/wiki/William_Clito" title="William Clito">William Clito</a>, and supported a major rebellion in the Duchy between 1116 and 1119. Following Henry\'s victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Br%C3%A9mule" title="Battle of Br\xc3\xa9mule">Battle of Br\xc3\xa9mule</a>, a favourable peace settlement was agreed with Louis in 1120.\n</p><p>Considered by contemporaries to be a harsh but effective ruler, Henry skilfully manipulated the barons in England and Normandy. In England, he drew on the existing <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon">Anglo-Saxon</a> system of justice, local government and taxation, but also strengthened it with additional institutions, including the royal <a href="/wiki/Exchequer" title="Exchequer">exchequer</a> and itinerant <a href="/wiki/Judge" title="Judge">justices</a>. Normandy was also governed through a growing system of justices and an exchequer. Many of the officials who ran Henry\'s system were "new men" of obscure backgrounds rather than from families of high status, who rose through the ranks as administrators. Henry encouraged <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_Reform" title="Gregorian Reform">ecclesiastical reform</a>, but became embroiled in a serious dispute in 1101 with Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm of Canterbury</a>, which was resolved through a compromise solution in 1105. He supported the <a href="/wiki/Cluniac" class="mw-redirect" title="Cluniac">Cluniac</a> order and played a major role in the selection of the senior clergy in England and Normandy.\n</p><p>Henry\'s only legitimate son and heir, <a href="/wiki/William_Adelin" title="William Adelin">William Adelin</a>, drowned in the <i><a href="/wiki/White_Ship" title="White Ship">White Ship</a></i> disaster of 1120, throwing the royal succession into doubt. Henry took a second wife, <a href="/wiki/Adeliza_of_Louvain" title="Adeliza of Louvain">Adeliza of Louvain</a>, in the hope of having another son, but their marriage was childless. In response to this, Henry declared his daughter, <a href="/wiki/Empress_Matilda" title="Empress Matilda">Empress Matilda</a>, his heir and married her to <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_of_Anjou" class="mw-redirect" title="Geoffrey of Anjou">Geoffrey of Anjou</a>. The relationship between Henry and the couple became strained, and fighting broke out along the border with Anjou. Henry died on 1 December 1135 after a week of illness. Despite his plans for Matilda, the King was succeeded by his nephew, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_of_Blois" class="mw-redirect" title="Stephen of Blois">Stephen of Blois</a>, resulting in a period of civil war known as <a href="/wiki/The_Anarchy" title="The Anarchy">the Anarchy</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" piety</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Later_reign,_1107\xe2\x80\x9335"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Later reign, 1107\xe2\x80\x9335</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Continental_and_Welsh_politics,_1108\xe2\x80\x9314"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Continental and Welsh politics, 1108\xe2\x80\x9314</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Rebellion,_1115\xe2\x80\x9320"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Rebellion, 1115\xe2\x80\x9320</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Succession_crisis,_1120\xe2\x80\x9323"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Succession crisis, 1120\xe2\x80\x9323</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Planning_the_succession,_1124\xe2\x80\x9334"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Planning the succession, 1124\xe2\x80\x9334</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Death_and_legacy"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Death and legacy</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Death,_1135"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Death, 1135</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Historiography"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Family_and_children"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Family and children</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Legitimate"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Legitimate</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Illegitimate"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Illegitimate</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-27"><a href="#Sons"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Sons</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-28"><a href="#Daughters"><span class="tocnumber">6.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Daughters</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Ancestors"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Ancestors</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span id="Early_life.2C_1068.E2.80.931099"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life,_1068\xe2\x80\x931099">Early life, 1068\xe2\x80\x931099</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life, 1068\xe2\x80\x931099">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Childhood_and_appearance.2C_1068.E2.80.9386"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_and_appearance,_1068\xe2\x80\x9386">Childhood and appearance, 1068\xe2\x80\x9386</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Childhood and appearance, 1068\xe2\x80\x9386">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Henry was probably born in England in 1068, in either the summer or the last weeks of the year, possibly in the town of <a href="/wiki/Selby" title="Selby">Selby</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterGreenP20_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterGreenP20-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup> His father was <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Normandy" title="Duke of Normandy">Duke of Normandy</a>, who had <a href="/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman invasion of England">invaded England in 1066</a> to become the <a href="/wiki/King_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="King of England">King of England</a>, establishing lands stretching into <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>. The invasion had created an Anglo-Norman elite, many with estates spread across both sides of the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> These Anglo-Norman barons typically had close links to the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">kingdom of France</a>, which was then a loose collection of counties and smaller polities, under only the minimal control of the king.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s mother, <a href="/wiki/Matilda_of_Flanders" title="Matilda of Flanders">Matilda of Flanders</a>, was the granddaughter of <a href="/wiki/Robert_II_of_France" title="Robert II of France">Robert II of France</a>, and she probably named Henry after her uncle, King <a href="/wiki/Henry_I_of_France" title="Henry I of France">Henry I of France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry was the youngest of William and Matilda\'s four sons. Physically he resembled his older brothers <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curthose" title="Robert Curthose">Robert Curthose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard,_Duke_of_Bernay" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard, Duke of Bernay">Richard</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_England" title="William II of England">William Rufus</a>, being, as historian David Carpenter describes, "short, stocky and barrel-chested," with black hair.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> As a result of their age differences and Richard\'s early death, Henry would have probably seen relatively little of his older brothers.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> He probably knew his sister, <a href="/wiki/Adela_of_Normandy" title="Adela of Normandy">Adela</a>, well, as the two were close in age.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> There is little documentary evidence for his early years; historians <a href="/wiki/C._Warren_Hollister" title="C. Warren Hollister">Warren Hollister</a> and Kathleen Thompson suggest he was brought up predominantly in England, while <a href="/wiki/Judith_Green_(historian)" title="Judith Green (historian)">Judith Green</a> argues he was initially brought up in the Duchy.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;nb 2&#93;</a></sup> He was probably educated by the Church, possibly by <a href="/wiki/Osmund_(bishop_of_Salisbury)" class="mw-redirect" title="Osmund (bishop of Salisbury)">Bishop Osmund</a>, the King\'s <a href="/wiki/Chancellor" title="Chancellor">chancellor</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Cathedral" title="Salisbury Cathedral">Salisbury Cathedral</a>; it is uncertain if this indicated an intent by his parents for Henry to become a member of the clergy.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterGreeenPP36P22_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterGreeenPP36P22-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;nb 3&#93;</a></sup> It is also uncertain how far Henry\'s education extended, but he was probably able to read <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and had some background in the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_arts" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal arts">liberal arts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> He was given military training by an instructor called Robert Achard, and Henry was knighted by his father on 24 May 1086.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Inheritance.2C_1087.E2.80.9388"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Inheritance,_1087\xe2\x80\x9388">Inheritance, 1087\xe2\x80\x9388</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Inheritance, 1087\xe2\x80\x9388">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_I_of_England.jpg" class="image"><img alt="13th century picture" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Henry_I_of_England.jpg/220px-Henry_I_of_England.jpg" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Henry_I_of_England.jpg/330px-Henry_I_of_England.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Henry_I_of_England.jpg/440px-Henry_I_of_England.jpg 2x" data-file-width="502" data-file-height="504" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_I_of_England.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>13th-century depiction of Henry</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1087, William was fatally injured during a campaign in the <a href="/wiki/Vexin" title="Vexin">Vexin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Henry joined his dying father near <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a> in September, where the King partitioned his possessions among his sons.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The rules of succession in western Europe at the time were uncertain; in some parts of France, <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a>, in which the eldest son would inherit a title, was growing in popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-BarlowP162_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BarlowP162-18">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In other parts of Europe, including Normandy and England, the tradition was for lands to be divided up, with the eldest son taking patrimonial lands \xe2\x80\x93 usually considered to be the most valuable \xe2\x80\x93 and younger sons given smaller, or more recently acquired, partitions or estates.<sup id="cite_ref-BarlowP162_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BarlowP162-18">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In dividing his lands, William appears to have followed the Norman tradition, distinguishing between Normandy, which he had inherited, and England, which he had acquired through war.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> William\'s second son, Richard, had died in a hunting accident, leaving Henry and his two brothers to inherit William\'s estate. Robert, the eldest, despite being in armed rebellion against his father at the time of his death, received Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003pp38_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003pp38-20">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> England was given to William Rufus, who was in favour with the dying king.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003pp38_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003pp38-20">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Henry was given a large sum of money, usually reported as \xc2\xa35,000, with the expectation that he would also be given his mother\'s modest set of lands in <a href="/wiki/Buckinghamshire" title="Buckinghamshire">Buckinghamshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gloucestershire" title="Gloucestershire">Gloucestershire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;nb 4&#93;</a></sup> William\'s funeral at <a href="/wiki/Caen" title="Caen">Caen</a> was marred by angry complaints from a local man, and Henry may have been responsible for resolving the dispute by buying off the protester with silver.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Robert returned to Normandy, expecting to have been given both the Duchy and England, to find that William Rufus had crossed the Channel and been crowned king, as William II.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P48_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P48-25">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The two brothers disagreed fundamentally over the inheritance, and Robert soon began to plan an invasion of England to seize the kingdom, helped by a rebellion by some of the leading nobles against William Rufus.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Henry remained in Normandy and took up a role within Robert\'s court, possibly either because he was unwilling to  side openly with William Rufus, or because Robert might have taken the opportunity to confiscate Henry\'s inherited money if he had tried to leave.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P48_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P48-25">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;nb 5&#93;</a></sup> William Rufus sequestered Henry\'s new estates in England, leaving Henry landless.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1088, Robert\'s plans for the invasion of England began to falter, and he turned to Henry, proposing that his brother lend him some of his inheritance, which Henry refused.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterP49_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterP49-30">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Henry and Robert then came to an alternative arrangement, in which Robert would make Henry the count of western Normandy, in exchange for \xc2\xa33,000.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterP49_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterP49-30">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;nb 6&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s lands were a new countship based around a delegation of the ducal authority in the <a href="/wiki/Cotentin" class="mw-redirect" title="Cotentin">Cotentin</a>, but it extended across the <a href="/wiki/Avranchin" title="Avranchin">Avranchin</a>, with control over the bishoprics of both.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> This also gave Henry influence over two major Norman leaders, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_d%27Avranches,_1st_Earl_of_Chester" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugh d&#39;Avranches, 1st Earl of Chester">Hugh d\'Avranches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_de_Redvers" title="Richard de Redvers">Richard de Redvers</a>, and the abbey of <a href="/wiki/Mont_Saint-Michel" class="mw-redirect" title="Mont Saint-Michel">Mont Saint-Michel</a>, whose lands spread out further across the Duchy.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Robert\'s invasion force failed to leave Normandy, leaving William Rufus secure in England.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Count_of_the_Cotentin.2C_1088.E2.80.9390"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Count_of_the_Cotentin,_1088\xe2\x80\x9390">Count of the Cotentin, 1088\xe2\x80\x9390</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Count of the Cotentin, 1088\xe2\x80\x9390">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Odo_bayeux_tapestry.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Odo_bayeux_tapestry.png/220px-Odo_bayeux_tapestry.png" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Odo_bayeux_tapestry.png/330px-Odo_bayeux_tapestry.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Odo_bayeux_tapestry.png/440px-Odo_bayeux_tapestry.png 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1088" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Odo_bayeux_tapestry.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of <a href="/wiki/Odo,_Earl_of_Kent" class="mw-redirect" title="Odo, Earl of Kent">Bishop Odo</a> (wielding club at centre) who imprisoned Henry from 1088\xe2\x80\x9389. From the <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Tapestry" title="Bayeux Tapestry">Bayeux Tapestry</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Henry quickly established himself as count, building up a network of followers from western Normandy and eastern Brittany, whom historian <a href="/wiki/John_Le_Patourel" title="John Le Patourel">John Le Patourel</a> has characterised as "Henry\'s gang".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> His early supporters included Roger of Mandeville, Richard of Redvers, <a href="/wiki/Richard_d%27Avranches,_2nd_Earl_of_Chester" title="Richard d&#39;Avranches, 2nd Earl of Chester">Richard d\'Avranches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fitzhamon" title="Robert Fitzhamon">Robert Fitzhamon</a>, along with the churchman <a href="/wiki/Roger_of_Salisbury" title="Roger of Salisbury">Roger of Salisbury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Robert attempted to go back on his deal with Henry and re-appropriate the county, but Henry\'s grip was already sufficiently firm to prevent this.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Robert\'s rule of the Duchy was chaotic, and parts of Henry\'s lands became almost independent of central control from Rouen.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During this period, neither William nor Robert seems to have trusted Henry.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Waiting until the rebellion against William Rufus was safely over, Henry returned to England in July 1088.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> He met with the King but was unable to persuade him to grant him their mother\'s estates, and travelled back to Normandy in the autumn.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> While he had been away, however, <a href="/wiki/Odo,_Earl_of_Kent" class="mw-redirect" title="Odo, Earl of Kent">Odo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Bayeux" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Bayeux">Bishop of Bayeux</a>, who regarded Henry as a potential competitor, had convinced Robert that Henry was conspiring against the duke with William Rufus.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> On landing, Odo seized Henry and imprisoned him in <a href="/wiki/Neuilly-la-For%C3%AAt" title="Neuilly-la-For\xc3\xaat">Neuilly-la-For\xc3\xaat</a>, and Robert took back the county of the Cotentin.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Henry was held there over the winter, but in the spring of 1089 the senior elements of the Normandy nobility prevailed upon Robert to release him.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although no longer formally the Count of Cotentin, Henry continued to control the west of Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The struggle between Henry\'s brothers continued. William Rufus continued to put down resistance to his rule in England, but began to build a number of alliances against Robert with barons in Normandy and neighbouring Ponthieu.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Robert allied himself with <a href="/wiki/Philip_I_of_France" title="Philip I of France">Philip I of France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In late 1090 William Rufus encouraged Conan Pilatus, a powerful burgher in Rouen, to rebel against Robert; Conan was supported by most of Rouen and made appeals to the neighbouring ducal garrisons to switch allegiance as well.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Robert issued an appeal for help to his barons, and Henry was the first to arrive in Rouen in November.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P71_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P71-50">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Violence broke out, leading to savage, confused street fighting as both sides attempted to take control of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P71_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P71-50">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Robert and Henry left the castle to join the battle, but Robert then retreated, leaving Henry to continue the fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P72_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P72-51">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The battle turned in favour of the ducal forces and Henry took Conan prisoner.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P72_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P72-51">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Henry was angry that Conan had turned against his feudal lord. He had him taken to the top of Rouen Castle and then, despite Conan\'s offers to pay a huge ransom, threw him off the top of the castle to his death.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Contemporaries considered Henry to have acted appropriately in making an example of Conan, and Henry became famous for his exploits in the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Fall_and_rise.2C_1091.E2.80.9399"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall_and_rise,_1091\xe2\x80\x9399">Fall and rise, 1091\xe2\x80\x9399</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Fall and rise, 1091\xe2\x80\x9399">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mont_St_Michel_3,_Brittany,_France_-_July_2011.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of Mont St Michel" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Mont_St_Michel_3%2C_Brittany%2C_France_-_July_2011.jpg/220px-Mont_St_Michel_3%2C_Brittany%2C_France_-_July_2011.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Mont_St_Michel_3%2C_Brittany%2C_France_-_July_2011.jpg/330px-Mont_St_Michel_3%2C_Brittany%2C_France_-_July_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Mont_St_Michel_3%2C_Brittany%2C_France_-_July_2011.jpg/440px-Mont_St_Michel_3%2C_Brittany%2C_France_-_July_2011.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2811" data-file-height="1993" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mont_St_Michel_3,_Brittany,_France_-_July_2011.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mont_Saint-Michel" class="mw-redirect" title="Mont Saint-Michel">Mont Saint-Michel</a>, site of the 1091 siege</div></div></div>\n<p>In the aftermath, Robert forced Henry to leave Rouen, probably because Henry\'s role in the fighting had been more prominent than his own, and possibly because Henry had asked to be formally reinstated as the count of the Cotentin.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In early 1091, William Rufus invaded Normandy with a sufficiently large army to bring Robert to the negotiating table.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003PP76_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003PP76-55">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The two brothers signed a treaty at Rouen, granting William Rufus a range of lands and castles in Normandy. In return, William Rufus promised to support Robert\'s attempts to regain control of the neighbouring county of Maine, once under Norman control, and help in regaining control over the Duchy, including Henry\'s lands.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003PP76_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003PP76-55">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> They nominated each other as heirs to England and Normandy, excluding Henry from any succession while either one of them lived.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>War now broke out between Henry and his brothers.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Henry mobilised a mercenary army in the west of Normandy, but as William Rufus and Robert\'s forces advanced, his network of baronial support melted away.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Henry focused his remaining forces at <a href="/wiki/Mont_Saint-Michel" class="mw-redirect" title="Mont Saint-Michel">Mont Saint-Michel</a>, where he was besieged, probably in March 1091.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The site was easy to defend, but lacked fresh water.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The chronicler <a href="/wiki/William_of_Malmesbury" title="William of Malmesbury">William of Malmesbury</a> suggested that when Henry\'s water ran short, Robert allowed his brother fresh supplies, leading to remonstrations between Robert and William Rufus.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The events of the final days of the siege are unclear: the besiegers had begun to argue about the future strategy for the campaign, but Henry then abandoned Mont Saint-Michel, probably as part of a negotiated surrender.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterP82GreenP32_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterP82GreenP32-62">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;nb 7&#93;</a></sup> He left for Brittany and crossed over into France.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry\'s next steps are not well documented; one chronicler, <a href="/wiki/Orderic_Vitalis" title="Orderic Vitalis">Orderic Vitalis</a>, suggests that he travelled in the French Vexin, along the Normandy border, for over a year with a small band of followers.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the year, Robert and William Rufus had fallen out once again, and the Treaty of Rouen had been abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> In 1092, Henry and his followers seized the Normandy town of <a href="/wiki/Domfront,_Orne" title="Domfront, Orne">Domfront</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Domfront had previously been controlled by <a href="/wiki/Robert_of_Bell%C3%AAme,_3rd_Earl_of_Shrewsbury" title="Robert of Bell\xc3\xaame, 3rd Earl of Shrewsbury">Robert of Bell\xc3\xaame</a>, but the inhabitants disliked his rule and invited Henry to take over the town, which he did in a bloodless coup.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Over the next two years, Henry re-established his network of supporters across western Normandy, forming what Judith Green terms a "court in waiting".<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> By 1094, he was allocating lands and castles to his followers as if he were the Duke of Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> William Rufus began to support Henry with money, encouraging his campaign against Robert, and Henry used some of this to construct a <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Domfront" title="Ch\xc3\xa2teau de Domfront">substantial castle</a> at Domfront.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>William Rufus crossed into Normandy to take the war to Robert in 1094, and when progress stalled, called upon Henry for assistance.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Henry responded, but travelled to London instead of joining the main campaign further east in Normandy, possibly at the request of the King, who in any event abandoned the campaign and returned to England.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;nb 8&#93;</a></sup> Over the next few years, Henry appears to have strengthened his power base in western Normandy, visiting England occasionally to attend at William Rufus\'s court.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In 1095 <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_II" title="Pope Urban II">Pope Urban II</a> called the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a>, encouraging knights from across Europe to join.<sup id="cite_ref-Green2009P35_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green2009P35-74">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Robert joined the Crusade, borrowing money from William Rufus to do so, and granting the King temporary custody of his part of the Duchy in exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The King appeared confident of regaining the remainder of Normandy from Robert, and Henry appeared ever closer to William Rufus, the pair campaigning together in the Norman Vexin between 1097 and 1098.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Early_reign.2C_1100.E2.80.9306"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_reign,_1100\xe2\x80\x9306">Early reign, 1100\xe2\x80\x9306</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early reign, 1100\xe2\x80\x9306">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Taking_the_throne.2C_1100"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Taking_the_throne,_1100">Taking the throne, 1100</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Taking the throne, 1100">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coronation_of_henry_i.png" class="image"><img alt="Claudius Pontificals" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Coronation_of_henry_i.png/170px-Coronation_of_henry_i.png" width="170" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Coronation_of_henry_i.png/255px-Coronation_of_henry_i.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Coronation_of_henry_i.png/340px-Coronation_of_henry_i.png 2x" data-file-width="361" data-file-height="493" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coronation_of_henry_i.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 17th-century <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Pontificals" title="Claudius Pontificals">manuscript</a> drawing of Henry\'s coronation.</div></div></div>\n<p>Henry became King of England following the death of William Rufus, who had been shot while hunting.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> On the afternoon of 2 August 1100, the King had gone hunting in the <a href="/wiki/New_Forest" title="New Forest">New Forest</a>, accompanied by a team of huntsmen and a number of the Norman nobility, including Henry.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> An arrow was fired, possibly by the baron Walter Tirel, which hit and killed William Rufus.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P103_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P103-81">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Numerous conspiracy theories have been put forward suggesting that the King was killed deliberately; most modern historians reject these, as hunting was a risky activity, and such accidents were common.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;nb 9&#93;</a></sup> Chaos broke out, and Tirel fled the scene for France, either because he had fired the fatal shot, or because he had been incorrectly accused and feared that he would be made a scapegoat for the King\'s death.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P103_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P103-81">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry rode to Winchester, where an argument ensued as to who now had the best claim to the throne.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> William of Breteuil championed the rights of Robert, who was still abroad, returning from the Crusade, and to whom Henry and the barons had given <a href="/wiki/Homage_(feudal)" title="Homage (feudal)">homage</a> in previous years.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Henry argued that, unlike Robert, he had been born to a reigning king and queen, thereby giving him a claim under the right of <a href="/wiki/Porphyrogeniture" title="Porphyrogeniture">porphyrogeniture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Tempers flared, but Henry, supported by Henry de Beaumont and Robert of Meulan, held sway and persuaded the barons to follow him.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> He occupied <a href="/wiki/Winchester_Castle" title="Winchester Castle">Winchester Castle</a> and seized the royal treasury.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry was hastily crowned king in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> on 5 August by <a href="/wiki/Maurice_(bishop_of_London)" title="Maurice (bishop of London)">Maurice</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_London" title="Bishop of London">Bishop of London</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, had been exiled by William Rufus, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_of_Bayeux" title="Thomas of Bayeux">Thomas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">Archbishop of York</a>, was in the north of England at <a href="/wiki/Ripon" title="Ripon">Ripon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In accordance with English tradition and in a bid to legitimise his rule, Henry issued a <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_Liberties" title="Charter of Liberties">coronation charter</a> laying out various commitments.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The new king presented himself as having restored order to a trouble-torn country.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> He announced that he would abandon William Rufus\'s policies towards the Church, which had been seen as oppressive by the clergy; he promised to prevent royal abuses of the barons\' property rights, and assured a return to the gentler customs of <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a>; he asserted that he would "establish a firm peace" across England and ordered "that this peace shall henceforth be kept".<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to his existing circle of supporters, many of whom were richly rewarded with new lands, Henry quickly co-opted many of the existing administration into his new royal household.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P116_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P116-94">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Giffard" title="William Giffard">William Giffard</a>, William Rufus\'s chancellor, was made the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Winchester" title="Bishop of Winchester">Bishop of Winchester</a>, and the prominent sheriffs <a href="/wiki/Urse_d%27Abetot" title="Urse d&#39;Abetot">Urse d\'Abetot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haimo_(dapifer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Haimo (dapifer)">Haimo Dapifer</a> and Robert Fitzhamon continued to play a senior role in government.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P116_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P116-94">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> By contrast, the unpopular <a href="/wiki/Ranulf_Flambard" title="Ranulf Flambard">Ranulf Flambard</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Durham" title="Bishop of Durham">Bishop of Durham</a> and a key member of the previous regime, was imprisoned in the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a> and charged with corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The late king had left many church positions unfilled, and Henry set about nominating candidates to these, in an effort to build further support for his new government.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The appointments needed to be consecrated, and Henry wrote to Anselm, apologising for having been crowned while the Archbishop was still in France and asking him to return at once.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Marriage_to_Matilda.2C_1100"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_to_Matilda,_1100">Marriage to Matilda, 1100</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Marriage to Matilda, 1100">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Matylda_zena.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Picture of Matilda" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Matylda_zena.jpg/220px-Matylda_zena.jpg" width="220" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2b/Matylda_zena.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="323" data-file-height="331" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Matylda_zena.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Henry\'s first wife, <a href="/wiki/Matilda_of_Scotland" title="Matilda of Scotland">Matilda of Scotland</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On 11 November 1100 Henry married <a href="/wiki/Matilda_of_Scotland" title="Matilda of Scotland">Matilda</a>, the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_III_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm III of Scotland">Malcolm III of Scotland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P130_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P130-98">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Henry was now around 31 years old, but late marriages for noblemen were not unusual in the 11th century.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The pair had probably first met earlier the previous decade, possibly being introduced through Bishop Osmund of Salisbury.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Historian Warren Hollister argues that Henry and Matilda were emotionally close, but their union was also certainly politically motivated.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;nb 10&#93;</a></sup> Matilda had originally been named Edith, an Anglo-Saxon name, and was a member of the West Saxon royal family, being the niece of <a href="/wiki/Edgar_the_%C3%86theling" class="mw-redirect" title="Edgar the \xc3\x86theling">Edgar the \xc3\x86theling</a>, the great-granddaughter of <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Ironside" title="Edmund Ironside">Edmund Ironside</a> and a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> For Henry, marrying Matilda gave his reign increased legitimacy, and for Matilda, an ambitious woman, it was an opportunity for high status and power in England.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Matilda had been educated in a sequence of convents, however, and may well have taken the vows to formally become a nun, which formed an obstacle to the marriage progressing.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterPP128-129_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterPP128-129-106">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> She did not wish to be a nun and appealed to Anselm for permission to marry Henry, and the Archbishop established a council at <a href="/wiki/Lambeth_Palace" title="Lambeth Palace">Lambeth Palace</a> to judge the issue.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterPP128-129_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterPP128-129-106">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Despite some dissenting voices, the council concluded that although Matilda had lived in a convent, she had not actually become a nun and was therefore free to marry, a judgement that Anselm then affirmed, allowing the marriage to proceed.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterPP128-129_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterPP128-129-106">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;nb 11&#93;</a></sup> Matilda proved an effective queen for Henry, acting as a regent in England on occasion, addressing and presiding over councils, and extensively supporting the arts.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The couple soon had two children, <a href="/wiki/Empress_Matilda" title="Empress Matilda">Matilda</a>, born in 1102, and <a href="/wiki/William_Adelin" title="William Adelin">William Adelin</a>, born in 1103; it is possible that they also had a second son, Richard, who died young.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;nb 12&#93;</a></sup> Following the birth of these children, Matilda preferred to remain based in Westminster while Henry travelled across England and Normandy, either for religious reasons or because she enjoyed being involved in the machinery of royal governance.<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson_2003_137_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson_2003_137-113">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry had a considerable sexual appetite and enjoyed a substantial number of sexual partners, resulting in a large number of illegitimate children, at least nine sons and 13 daughters, many of whom he appears to have recognised and supported.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> It was normal for unmarried Anglo-Norman noblemen to have sexual relations with prostitutes and local women, and kings were also expected to have mistresses.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterP45ThompsonP135_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterP45ThompsonP135-115">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;nb 13&#93;</a></sup> Some of these relationships occurred before Henry was married, but many others took place after his marriage to Matilda.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Henry had a wide range of mistresses from a range of backgrounds, and the relationships appear to have been conducted relatively openly.<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson_2003_137_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson_2003_137-113">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> He may have chosen some of his noble mistresses for political purposes, but the evidence to support this theory is limited.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Treaty_of_Alton.2C_1101.E2.80.9302"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Treaty_of_Alton,_1101\xe2\x80\x9302">Treaty of Alton, 1101\xe2\x80\x9302</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Treaty of Alton, 1101\xe2\x80\x9302">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_I_Cotton_Claudius_D._ii,_f._45v..jpg" class="image"><img alt="Drawing of Henry" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Henry_I_Cotton_Claudius_D._ii%2C_f._45v..jpg/220px-Henry_I_Cotton_Claudius_D._ii%2C_f._45v..jpg" width="220" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Henry_I_Cotton_Claudius_D._ii%2C_f._45v..jpg/330px-Henry_I_Cotton_Claudius_D._ii%2C_f._45v..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Henry_I_Cotton_Claudius_D._ii%2C_f._45v..jpg/440px-Henry_I_Cotton_Claudius_D._ii%2C_f._45v..jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="917" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_I_Cotton_Claudius_D._ii,_f._45v..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early 14th-century depiction of Henry</div></div></div>\n<p>By early 1101, Henry\'s new regime was established and functioning, but many of the Anglo-Norman elite still supported Robert, or would be prepared to switch sides if Henry\'s elder brother appeared likely to gain power in England.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In February, Flambard escaped from the Tower of London and crossed the Channel to Normandy, where he injected fresh direction and energy to Robert\'s attempts to mobilise an invasion force.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> By July, Robert had formed an army and a fleet, ready to move against Henry in England.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Raising the stakes in the conflict, Henry seized Flambard\'s lands and, with the support of Anselm, Flambard was removed from his position as bishop.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Henry held court in April and June, where the nobility renewed their oaths of allegiance to him, but their support still appeared partial and shaky.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the invasion imminent, Henry mobilised his forces and fleet outside <a href="/wiki/Pevensey" title="Pevensey">Pevensey</a>, close to Robert\'s anticipated landing site, training some of them personally in how to counter cavalry charges.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Despite English levies and knights owing military service to the Church arriving in considerable numbers, many of his barons did not appear.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Anselm intervened with some of the doubters, emphasising the religious importance of their loyalty to Henry.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Robert unexpectedly landed further up the coast at <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth" title="Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a> on 20 July with a modest force of a few hundred men, but these were quickly joined by many of the barons in England.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> However, instead of marching into nearby Winchester and seizing Henry\'s treasury, Robert paused, giving Henry time to march west and intercept the invasion force.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003PP139_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003PP139-128">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The two armies met at <a href="/wiki/Alton,_Hampshire" title="Alton, Hampshire">Alton</a> where peace negotiations began, possibly initiated by either Henry or Robert, and probably supported by Flambard.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003PP139_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003PP139-128">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The brothers then agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Alton" title="Treaty of Alton">Treaty of Alton</a>, under which Robert released Henry from his oath of homage and recognised him as king; Henry renounced his claims on western Normandy, except for Domfront, and agreed to pay Robert \xc2\xa32,000 a year for life; if either brother died without a male heir, the other would inherit his lands; the barons whose lands had been seized by either the King or the Duke for supporting his rival would have them returned, and Flambard would be reinstated as bishop; the two brothers would campaign together to defend their territories in Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;nb 14&#93;</a></sup> Robert remained in England for a few months more with Henry before returning to Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite the treaty, Henry set about inflicting severe penalties on the barons who had stood against him during the invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_de_Warenne,_2nd_Earl_of_Surrey" title="William de Warenne, 2nd Earl of Surrey">William de Warenne</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Surrey" title="Earl of Surrey">Earl of Surrey</a>, was accused of fresh crimes, which were not covered by the Alton amnesty, and was banished from England.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> In 1102 Henry then turned against Robert of Bell\xc3\xaame and his brothers, the most powerful of the barons, accusing him of 45 different offences.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Robert escaped and took up arms against Henry.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Henry besieged Robert\'s castles at <a href="/wiki/Arundel_Castle" title="Arundel Castle">Arundel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tickhill_Castle" title="Tickhill Castle">Tickhill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury_Castle" title="Shrewsbury Castle">Shrewsbury</a>, pushing down into the south-west to attack <a href="/wiki/Bridgnorth" title="Bridgnorth">Bridgnorth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> His power base in England broken, Robert accepted Henry\'s offer of banishment and left the country for Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Conquest_of_Normandy.2C_1103.E2.80.9306"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Conquest_of_Normandy,_1103\xe2\x80\x9306">Conquest of Normandy, 1103\xe2\x80\x9306</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Conquest of Normandy, 1103\xe2\x80\x9306">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tinchebray_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of Tinchebray" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Tinchebray_01.jpg/220px-Tinchebray_01.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Tinchebray_01.jpg/330px-Tinchebray_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Tinchebray_01.jpg/440px-Tinchebray_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tinchebray_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The village of Tinchebray in 2008</div></div></div>\n<p>Henry\'s network of allies in Normandy became stronger during 1103.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Henry married Juliana, one of his illegitimate daughters, to Eustace of Breteuil, and another illegitimate daughter, Matilda, to <a href="/wiki/Rotrou_III,_Count_of_Perche" title="Rotrou III, Count of Perche">Rotrou</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Count_of_Perche" class="mw-redirect" title="Count of Perche">Count of Perche</a>, on the Normandy border.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Henry attempted to win over other members of the Normandy nobility and gave other English estates and lucrative offers to key Norman lords.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Duke Robert continued to fight Robert of Bell\xc3\xaame, but the Duke\'s position worsened, until by 1104, he had to ally himself formally with Bell\xc3\xaame to survive.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Arguing that Duke Robert had broken the terms of their treaty, Henry crossed over the Channel to Domfront, where he met with senior barons from across Normandy, eager to ally themselves with the King.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Henry confronted his brother and accused him of siding with his enemies, before returning to England.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Normandy continued to disintegrate into chaos.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P185_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P185-145">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> In 1105, Henry sent his friend Robert Fitzhamon and a force of knights into the Duchy, apparently to provoke a confrontation with Duke Robert.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Fitzhamon was captured, and Henry used this as an excuse to invade, promising to restore peace and order.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P185_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P185-145">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Henry had the support of most of the neighbouring counts around Normandy\'s borders, and King Philip of France was persuaded to remain neutral.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Henry occupied western Normandy, and advanced east on Bayeux, where Fitzhamon was held.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P188_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P188-148">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> The city refused to surrender, and Henry besieged it, burning it to the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P188_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P188-148">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Terrified of meeting the same fate, the town of Caen switched sides and surrendered, allowing Henry to advance on <a href="/wiki/Falaise,_Calvados" title="Falaise, Calvados">Falaise</a>, which he took with some casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s campaign stalled, and the King instead began peace discussions with Robert.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The negotiations were inconclusive and the fighting dragged on until Christmas, when Henry returned to England.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry invaded again in July 1106, hoping to provoke a decisive battle.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> After some initial tactical successes, he turned south-west towards the castle of <a href="/wiki/Tinchebray" title="Tinchebray">Tinchebray</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister203P199_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister203P199-153">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> He besieged the castle and Duke Robert, supported by Robert of Bell\xc3\xaame, advanced from Falaise to relieve it.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister203P199_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister203P199-153">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> After attempts at negotiation failed, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tinchebray" title="Battle of Tinchebray">Battle of Tinchebray</a> took place, probably on 28 September.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;nb 15&#93;</a></sup> The battle lasted around an hour, and began with a charge by Duke Robert\'s cavalry; the infantry and dismounted knights of both sides then joined the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s reserves, led by <a href="/wiki/Elias_I,_Count_of_Maine" title="Elias I, Count of Maine">Elias</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Count_of_Maine" class="mw-redirect" title="Count of Maine">Count of Maine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_IV,_Duke_of_Brittany" title="Alan IV, Duke of Brittany">Alan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Brittany" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Brittany">Duke of Brittany</a>, attacked the enemy\'s flanks, routing first Bell\xc3\xaame\'s troops and then the bulk of the ducal forces.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P201_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P201-158">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Duke Robert was taken prisoner, but Bell\xc3\xaame escaped.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P201_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P201-158">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry mopped up the remaining resistance in Normandy, and Robert ordered his last garrisons to surrender.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Reaching Rouen, Henry reaffirmed the laws and customs of Normandy and took homage from the leading barons and citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The lesser prisoners taken at Tinchebray were released, but Robert and several other leading nobles were imprisoned indefinitely.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s nephew, Robert\'s son <a href="/wiki/William_Clito" title="William Clito">William Clito</a>, was only three years old and was released to the care of <a href="/wiki/Helias_of_Saint-Saens" title="Helias of Saint-Saens">Helias of Saint-Saens</a>, a Norman baron.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Henry reconciled himself with Robert of Bell\xc3\xaame, who gave up the ducal lands he had seized and rejoined the royal court.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Henry had no way of legally removing the Duchy from his brother Robert, and initially Henry avoided using the title "duke" at all, emphasising that, as the King of England, he was only acting as the guardian of the troubled Duchy.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Government.2C_family_and_household"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Government,_family_and_household">Government, family and household</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Government, family and household">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Government.2C_law_and_court"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Government,_law_and_court">Government, law and court</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Government, law and court">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:403px;max-width:403px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:197px;max-width:197px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry1_seal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Henry1_seal.jpg/195px-Henry1_seal.jpg" width="195" height="196" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Henry1_seal.jpg/293px-Henry1_seal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/01/Henry1_seal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="315" data-file-height="317" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:HenryI.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/HenryI.jpg/200px-HenryI.jpg" width="200" height="201" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/HenryI.jpg/300px-HenryI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b9/HenryI.jpg 2x" data-file-width="337" data-file-height="339" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Henry\'s royal seal, showing the King on horseback (l) and seated on his throne (r)</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information&#32;on English government during this period: <a href="/wiki/England_in_the_Middle_Ages#High_Middle_Ages_(1066\xe2\x80\x931272)" title="England in the Middle Ages">England in the Middle Ages \xc2\xa7&#160;High Middle Ages (1066\xe2\x80\x931272)</a></div>\n<p>Henry inherited the kingdom of England from William Rufus, giving him a claim of <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a> over Wales and Scotland, and acquired the Duchy of Normandy, a complex entity with troubled borders.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> The borders between England and Scotland were still uncertain during Henry\'s reign, with Anglo-Norman influence pushing northwards through <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a>, but Henry\'s relationship with King <a href="/wiki/David_I_of_Scotland" title="David I of Scotland">David I of Scotland</a> was generally good, partially due to Henry\'s marriage to his sister.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In Wales, Henry used his power to coerce and charm the indigenous Welsh princes, while Norman <a href="/wiki/Marcher_Lord" title="Marcher Lord">Marcher Lords</a> pushed across the valleys of South Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Normandy was controlled via various interlocking networks of ducal, ecclesiastical and family contacts, backed by a growing string of important ducal castles along the borders.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Alliances and relationships with neighbouring counties along the Norman border were particularly important to maintaining the stability of the Duchy.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry ruled through the various barons and lords in England and Normandy, whom he manipulated skilfully for political effect.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Political friendships, termed <i>amicitia</i> in Latin, were important during the 12th century, and Henry maintained a wide range of these, mediating between his friends in various factions across his realm when necessary, and rewarding those who were loyal to him.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Henry also had a reputation for punishing those barons who stood against him, and he maintained an effective network of informers and spies who reported to him on events.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Henry was a harsh, firm ruler, but not excessively so by the standards of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Over time, he increased the degree of his control over the barons, removing his enemies and bolstering his friends until the "reconstructed baronage", as historian Warren Hollister describes it, was predominantly loyal and dependent on the King.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry\'s itinerant royal court comprised various parts.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> At the heart was Henry\'s domestic household, called the <i>domus</i>; a wider grouping was termed the <i><a href="/wiki/Familia_regis" class="mw-redirect" title="Familia regis">familia regis</a></i>, and formal gatherings of the court were termed <i><a href="/wiki/Curia_regis" title="Curia regis">curia</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The <i>domus</i> was divided into several parts. The chapel, headed by the chancellor, looked after the royal documents, the chamber dealt with financial affairs and the master-marshal was responsible for travel and accommodation.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The <i>familia regis</i> included Henry\'s mounted household troops, up to several hundred strong, who came from a wider range of social backgrounds, and could be deployed across England and Normandy as required.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Initially Henry continued his father\'s practice of regular crown-wearing ceremonies at his <i>curia</i>, but they became less frequent as the years passed.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s court was grand and ostentatious, financing the construction of large new buildings and castles with a range of precious gifts on display, including the King\'s private menagerie of exotic animals, which he kept at <a href="/wiki/Woodstock_Palace" title="Woodstock Palace">Woodstock Palace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Despite being a lively community, Henry\'s court was more tightly controlled than those of previous kings.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003PP330_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003PP330-181">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Strict rules controlled personal behaviour and prohibited members of the court from pillaging neighbouring villages, as had been the norm under William Rufus.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003PP330_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003PP330-181">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry was responsible for a substantial expansion of the royal justice system.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;nb 16&#93;</a></sup> In England, Henry drew on the existing <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_law" title="Anglo-Saxon law">Anglo-Saxon system</a> of justice, local government and <a href="/wiki/Taxation_in_medieval_England#Anglo-Saxon_England_(597\xe2\x80\x931066)" title="Taxation in medieval England">taxes</a>, but strengthened it with additional central governmental institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Roger of Salisbury began to develop the royal exchequer after 1110, using it to collect and audit revenues from the King\'s sheriffs in the shires.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Itinerant justices began to emerge under Henry, travelling around the country managing <a href="/wiki/Eyre_(legal_term)" title="Eyre (legal term)">eyre courts</a>, and many more laws were formally recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Henry gathered increasing revenue from the expansion of royal justice, both from fines and from fees.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Pipe_Roll" class="mw-redirect" title="Pipe Roll">Pipe Roll</a> that is known to have survived dates from 1130, recording royal expenditures.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Henry reformed the coinage in 1107, 1108 and in 1125, inflicting harsh corporal punishments to English coiners who had been found guilty of debasing the currency.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;nb 17&#93;</a></sup> In Normandy, Henry restored law and order after 1106, operating through a body of Norman justices and an exchequer system similar to that in England.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Norman institutions grew in scale and scope under Henry, although less quickly than in England.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Many of the officials that ran Henry\'s system were termed "new men", relatively low-born individuals who rose through the ranks as administrators, managing justice or the royal revenues.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;nb 18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_the_church">Relations with the church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Relations with the church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information&#32;on religion in England during this period: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Medieval_England" title="Religion in Medieval England">Religion in Medieval England</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_and_the_King">Church and the King</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Church and the King">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anselm_of_Canterbury,_seal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Picture of seal" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Anselm_of_Canterbury%2C_seal.jpg/220px-Anselm_of_Canterbury%2C_seal.jpg" width="220" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/72/Anselm_of_Canterbury%2C_seal.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="257" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anselm_of_Canterbury,_seal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The seal of Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm of Canterbury</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Henry\'s ability to govern was intimately bound up with the Church, which formed the key to the administration of both England and Normandy, and this relationship changed considerably over the course of his reign.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> William the Conqueror had reformed the English Church with the support of his Archbishop of Canterbury, <a href="/wiki/Lanfranc" title="Lanfranc">Lanfranc</a>, who became a close colleague and advisor to the King.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;nb 19&#93;</a></sup> Under William Rufus this arrangement had collapsed, the King and Archbishop Anselm had become estranged and Anselm had gone into exile.
