Henry also believed in Church reform, but on taking power in England he became embroiled in the <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury#Conflicts_with_King_Henry_I" title="Anselm of Canterbury">investiture controversy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The argument concerned who should invest a new bishop with his staff and ring: traditionally, this had been carried out by the king in a symbolic demonstration of royal power, but Pope Urban II had condemned this practice in 1099, arguing that only the papacy could carry out this task, and declaring that the clergy should not give homage to their local temporal rulers.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Anselm returned to England from exile in 1100 having heard Urban\'s pronouncement, and informed Henry that he would be complying with the Pope\'s wishes.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Henry was in a difficult position. On one hand, the symbolism and homage was important to him; on the other hand, he needed Anselm\'s support in his struggle with his brother Duke Robert.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Anselm stuck firmly to the letter of the papal decree, despite Henry\'s attempts to persuade him to give way in return for a vague assurance of a future royal compromise.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Matters escalated, with Anselm going back into exile and Henry confiscating the revenues of his estates. Anselm threatened excommunication, and in July 1105 the two men finally negotiated a solution.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> A distinction was drawn between the secular and ecclesiastical powers of the prelates, under which Henry gave up his right to invest his clergy, but retained the custom of requiring them to come and do homage for the <a href="/wiki/Temporalities" title="Temporalities">temporalities</a>, the landed properties they held in England.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Despite this argument, the pair worked closely together, combining to deal with Duke Robert\'s invasion of 1101, for example, and holding major reforming councils in 1102 and 1108.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A long-running dispute between the Archbishops of Canterbury and York flared up under Anselm\'s successor, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_d%27Escures" title="Ralph d&#39;Escures">Ralph d\'Escures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MayrHarting2001PP58_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MayrHarting2001PP58-210">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Canterbury, traditionally the senior of the two establishments, had long argued that the Archbishop of York should formally promise to obey their Archbishop, but York argued that the two episcopates were independent within the English Church and that no such promise was necessary. Henry supported the primacy of Canterbury, to ensure that England remained under a single ecclesiastical administration, but the Pope preferred the case of York.<sup id="cite_ref-MayrHarting2001PP58_210-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MayrHarting2001PP58-210">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> The matter was complicated by Henry\'s personal friendship with <a href="/wiki/Thurstan" title="Thurstan">Thurstan</a>, the Archbishop of York, and the King\'s desire that the case should not end up in a papal court, beyond royal control.<sup id="cite_ref-MayrHarting2001PP58_210-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MayrHarting2001PP58-210">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Henry badly needed the support of the Papacy in his struggle with Louis of France, however, and therefore allowed Thurstan to attend the Council of Rheims in 1119, where Thurstan was then consecrated by the Pope with no mention of any duty towards Canterbury.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Henry believed that this went against assurances Thurstan had previously made and exiled him from England until the King and Archbishop came to a negotiated solution the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Even after the investiture dispute, the King continued to play a major role in the selection of new English and Norman bishops and archbishops.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> Henry appointed many of his officials to bishoprics and, as historian Martin Brett suggests, "some of his officers could look forward to a mitre with all but absolute confidence".<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s chancellors, and those of his queens, became bishops of Durham, Hereford, London, Lincoln, Winchester and Salisbury.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Henry increasingly drew on a wider range of these bishops as advisors \xe2\x80\x93 particularly Roger of Salisbury \xe2\x80\x93 breaking with the earlier tradition of relying primarily on the Archbishop of Canterbury.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> The result was a cohesive body of administrators through which Henry could exercise careful influence, holding general councils to discuss key matters of policy.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> This stability shifted slightly after 1125, when Henry began to inject a wider range of candidates into the senior positions of the Church, often with more reformist views, and the impact of this generation would be felt in the years after Henry\'s death.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_beliefs_and_piety">Personal beliefs and piety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Personal beliefs and piety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reading_Abbey_03.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of Reading Abbey" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Reading_Abbey_03.jpg/220px-Reading_Abbey_03.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Reading_Abbey_03.jpg/330px-Reading_Abbey_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Reading_Abbey_03.jpg/440px-Reading_Abbey_03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="685" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reading_Abbey_03.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ruined chapter house of <a href="/wiki/Reading_Abbey" title="Reading Abbey">Reading Abbey</a> in 2008</div></div></div>\n<p>Like other rulers of the period, Henry donated to the Church and patronised various religious communities, but contemporary chroniclers did not consider him an unusually pious king.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> His personal beliefs and piety may, however, have developed during the course of his life. Henry had always taken an interest in religion, but in his later years he may have become much more concerned about spiritual affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> If so, the major shifts in his thinking would appear to have occurred after 1120, when his son William Adelin died, and 1129, when his daughter\'s marriage teetered on the verge of collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;nb 20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a proponent of religious reform, Henry gave extensively to reformist groups within the Church.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> He was a keen supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Cluniac_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Cluniac order">Cluniac order</a>, probably for intellectual reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-Green2009P278_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green2009P278-225">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> He donated money to the abbey at Cluny itself, and after 1120 gave generously to <a href="/wiki/Reading_Abbey" title="Reading Abbey">Reading Abbey</a>, a Cluniac establishment.<sup id="cite_ref-Green2009P278_225-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green2009P278-225">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Construction on Reading began in 1121, and Henry endowed it with rich lands and extensive privileges, making it a symbol of his dynastic lines.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> He also focused effort on promoting the conversion of communities of clerks into Augustinian canons, the foundation of <a href="/wiki/Leper_hospital" class="mw-redirect" title="Leper hospital">leper hospitals</a>, expanding the provision of nunneries, and the charismatic orders of the <a href="/wiki/Congregation_of_Savigny" title="Congregation of Savigny">Savigniacs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tironensian_Order" title="Tironensian Order">Tironensians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> He was an avid collector of relics, sending an embassy to Constantinople in 1118 to collect Byzantine items, some of which were donated to Reading Abbey.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Later_reign.2C_1107.E2.80.9335"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_reign,_1107\xe2\x80\x9335">Later reign, 1107\xe2\x80\x9335</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Later reign, 1107\xe2\x80\x9335">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Continental_and_Welsh_politics.2C_1108.E2.80.9314"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Continental_and_Welsh_politics,_1108\xe2\x80\x9314">Continental and Welsh politics, 1108\xe2\x80\x9314</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Continental and Welsh politics, 1108\xe2\x80\x9314">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Normandy faced an increased threat from France, Anjou and Flanders after 1108.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P221_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P221-229">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Louis_VI_of_France" title="Louis VI of France">Louis VI</a> succeeded to the French throne in 1108 and began to reassert central royal power.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P221_229-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P221-229">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Louis demanded Henry give homage to him and that two disputed castles along the Normandy border be placed into the control of neutral castellans.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Henry refused, and Louis responded by mobilising an army.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P223_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P223-231">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> After some arguments, the two kings negotiated a truce and retreated without fighting, leaving the underlying issues unresolved.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P223_231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P223-231">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;nb 21&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fulk,_King_of_Jerusalem" title="Fulk, King of Jerusalem">Fulk V</a> assumed power in Anjou in 1109 and began to rebuild Angevin authority.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Fulk also inherited the county of Maine, but refused to recognise Henry as his feudal lord and instead allied himself with Louis.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Robert II of Flanders also briefly joined the alliance, before his death in 1111.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Louis_VI_denier_Bourges_1108_1137.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of coin" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Louis_VI_denier_Bourges_1108_1137.jpg/220px-Louis_VI_denier_Bourges_1108_1137.jpg" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Louis_VI_denier_Bourges_1108_1137.jpg/330px-Louis_VI_denier_Bourges_1108_1137.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Louis_VI_denier_Bourges_1108_1137.jpg/440px-Louis_VI_denier_Bourges_1108_1137.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1349" data-file-height="1354" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Louis_VI_denier_Bourges_1108_1137.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/French_denier" title="French denier">Denier coin</a> of Henry\'s rival, <a href="/wiki/Louis_VI_of_France" title="Louis VI of France">Louis VI of France</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1108, Henry betrothed his six-year-old daughter, Matilda, to <a href="/wiki/Henry_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry V, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry V</a>, the future <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> For King Henry, this was a prestigious match; for Henry V, it was an opportunity to restore his financial situation and fund an expedition to Italy, as he received a dowry of \xc2\xa36,666 from England and Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;nb 22&#93;</a></sup> Raising this money proved challenging, and required the implementation of a special "aid", or tax, in England.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Matilda was crowned Henry V\'s queen in 1110.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry responded to the French and Angevin threat by expanding his own network of supporters beyond the Norman borders.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Some Norman barons deemed unreliable were arrested or dispossessed, and Henry used their forfeited estates to bribe his potential allies in the neighbouring territories, in particular Maine.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Around 1110, Henry attempted to arrest the young William Clito, but William\'s mentors moved him to the safety of Flanders before he could be taken.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> At about this time, Henry probably began to style himself as the Duke of Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;nb 23&#93;</a></sup> Robert of Bell\xc3\xaame turned against Henry once again, and when he appeared at Henry\'s court in 1112 in a new role as a French ambassador, he was arrested and imprisoned.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Rebellions broke out in France and Anjou between 1111 and 1113, and Henry crossed into Normandy to support his nephew, Count <a href="/wiki/Theobald_II,_Count_of_Champagne" title="Theobald II, Count of Champagne">Theobald of Blois</a>, who had sided against Louis in the uprising.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> In a bid to diplomatically isolate the French King, Henry betrothed his young son, William Adelin, to Fulk\'s daughter Matilda, and married his illegitimate daughter Matilda to <a href="/wiki/Conan_III,_Duke_of_Brittany" title="Conan III, Duke of Brittany">Conan III</a>, the Duke of Brittany, creating alliances with Anjou and Brittany respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Louis backed down and in March 1113 met with Henry near Gisors to agree a peace settlement, giving Henry the disputed fortresses and confirming Henry\'s overlordship of Maine, Bell\xc3\xaame and Brittany.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, the situation in Wales was deteriorating. Henry had conducted a campaign in South Wales in 1108, pushing out royal power in the region and colonising the area around Pembroke with Flemings.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> By 1114, some of the resident Norman lords were under attack, while in Mid-Wales, <a href="/wiki/Owain_ap_Cadwgan" title="Owain ap Cadwgan">Owain ap Cadwgan</a> blinded one of the political hostages he was holding, and in North Wales <a href="/wiki/Gruffudd_ap_Cynan" title="Gruffudd ap Cynan">Gruffudd ap Cynan</a> threatened the power of the Earl of Chester.<sup id="cite_ref-Green2009P132_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green2009P132-254">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Henry sent three armies into Wales that year, with <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Fitz_Richard" title="Gilbert Fitz Richard">Gilbert Fitz Richard</a> leading a force from the south, Alexander, King of Scotland, pressing from the north and Henry himself advancing into Mid-Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-Green2009P132_254-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green2009P132-254">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Owain and Gruffudd sued for peace, and Henry accepted a political compromise.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> Henry reinforced the Welsh Marches with his own appointees, strengthening the border territories.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Rebellion.2C_1115.E2.80.9320"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebellion,_1115\xe2\x80\x9320">Rebellion, 1115\xe2\x80\x9320</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Rebellion, 1115\xe2\x80\x9320">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_I_coins.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of coin" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Henry_I_coins.jpg/220px-Henry_I_coins.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Henry_I_coins.jpg/330px-Henry_I_coins.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Henry_I_coins.jpg/440px-Henry_I_coins.jpg 2x" data-file-width="607" data-file-height="436" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_I_coins.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver <a href="/wiki/Penny_(English_coin)" title="Penny (English coin)">pennies</a> of Henry I, struck at the <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> <a href="/wiki/Mint_(coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mint (coin)">mint</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Concerned about the succession, Henry sought to persuade Louis VI to accept his son, William Adelin, as the legitimate future Duke of Normandy, in exchange for his son\'s homage.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> Henry crossed into Normandy in 1115 and assembled the Norman barons to swear loyalty; he also almost successfully negotiated a settlement with King Louis, affirming William\'s right to the Duchy in exchange for a large sum of money, but the deal fell through and Louis, backed by his ally Baldwin of Flanders, instead declared that he considered William Clito the legitimate heir to the Duchy.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>War broke out after Henry returned to Normandy with an army to support Theobald of Blois, who was under attack from Louis.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterP246GreenP135_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterP246GreenP135-259">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Henry and Louis raided each other\'s towns along the border, and a wider conflict then broke out, probably in 1116.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterP246GreenP135_259-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterP246GreenP135-259">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;nb 24&#93;</a></sup> Henry was pushed onto the defensive as French, Flemish and Angevin forces began to pillage the Normandy countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003Green2009PP246_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003Green2009PP246-262">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amaury_III_of_Montfort" title="Amaury III of Montfort">Amaury III of Montfort</a> and many other barons rose up against Henry, and there was an assassination plot from within his own household.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003Green2009PP246_262-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003Green2009PP246-262">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s wife, Matilda, died in early 1118, but the situation in Normandy was sufficiently pressing that Henry was unable to return to England for her funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry responded by mounting campaigns against the rebel barons and deepening his alliance with Theobald.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Baldwin of Flanders was wounded in battle and died in September 1118, easing the pressure on Normandy from the north-east.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> Henry attempted to crush a revolt in the city of <a href="/wiki/Alen%C3%A7on" title="Alen\xc3\xa7on">Alen\xc3\xa7on</a>, but was defeated by Fulk and the Angevin army.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Forced to retreat from Alen\xc3\xa7on, Henry\'s position deteriorated alarmingly, as his resources became overstretched and more barons abandoned his cause.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Early in 1119, Eustace of Breteuil and Henry\'s daughter, Juliana, threatened to join the baronial revolt.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P253_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P253-268">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Hostages were exchanged in a bid to avoid conflict, but relations broke down and both sides mutilated their captives.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterPP253-254_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterPP253-254-269">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Henry attacked and took the town of <a href="/wiki/Breteuil,_Eure" title="Breteuil, Eure">Breteuil</a>, despite Juliana\'s attempt to kill her father with a <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HollisterPP253-254_269-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HollisterPP253-254-269">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;nb 25&#93;</a></sup> In the aftermath, Henry dispossessed the couple of almost all of their lands in Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry\'s situation improved in May 1119 when he enticed Fulk to switch sides by finally agreeing to marry William Adelin to Fulk\'s daughter, <a href="/wiki/Matilda_of_Anjou" title="Matilda of Anjou">Matilda</a>, and paying Fulk a large sum of money.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Fulk left for the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, leaving the County of Maine in Henry\'s care, and the King was free to focus on crushing his remaining enemies.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> During the summer Henry advanced into the Norman Vexin, where he encountered Louis\'s army, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Br%C3%A9mule" title="Battle of Br\xc3\xa9mule">Battle of Br\xc3\xa9mule</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Henry appears to have deployed scouts and then organised his troops into several carefully formed lines of dismounted knights.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P264_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P264-276">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> Unlike Henry\'s forces, the French knights remained mounted; they hastily charged the Anglo-Norman positions, breaking through the first rank of the defences but then becoming entangled in Henry\'s second line of knights.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> Surrounded, the French army began to collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P264_276-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P264-276">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Melee" title="Melee">melee</a>, Henry was hit by a sword blow, but his armour protected him.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> Louis and William Clito escaped from the battle, leaving Henry to return to Rouen in triumph.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The war slowly petered out after this battle, and Louis took the dispute over Normandy to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Callixtus_II" title="Pope Callixtus II">Pope Callixtus II</a>\'s council in <a href="/wiki/Reims" title="Reims">Reims</a> that October.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> Henry faced a number of French complaints concerning his acquisition and subsequent management of Normandy, and despite being defended by <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Brito" title="Geoffrey Brito">Geoffrey</a>, the Archbishop of Rouen, Henry\'s case was shouted down by the pro-French elements of the council.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Callixtus declined to support Louis, however, and merely advised the two rulers to seek peace.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> Amaury de Montfort came to terms with Henry, but Henry and William Clito failed to find a mutually satisfactory compromise.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> In June 1120, Henry and Louis formally made peace on terms advantageous to the King of England: William Adelin gave homage to Louis, and in return Louis confirmed William\'s rights to the Duchy.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Succession_crisis.2C_1120.E2.80.9323"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession_crisis,_1120\xe2\x80\x9323">Succession crisis, 1120\xe2\x80\x9323</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Succession crisis, 1120\xe2\x80\x9323">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WhiteShipSinking.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Picture of White Ship" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/WhiteShipSinking.jpg/220px-WhiteShipSinking.jpg" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/45/WhiteShipSinking.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="262" data-file-height="334" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WhiteShipSinking.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early 14th-century depiction of the sinking of the <i><a href="/wiki/White_Ship" title="White Ship">White Ship</a></i> on 25 November 1120</div></div></div>\n<p>Henry\'s succession plans were thrown into chaos by the sinking of the <i><a href="/wiki/White_Ship" title="White Ship">White Ship</a></i> on 25 November 1120.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> Henry had left the port of Barfleur for England in the early evening, leaving William Adelin and many of the younger members of the court to follow on that night in a separate vessel, the <i>White Ship</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Both the crew and passengers were drunk and, just outside the harbour, the ship hit a submerged rock.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister203PP277_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister203PP277-287">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;nb 26&#93;</a></sup> The ship sank, killing as many as 300 people, with only one survivor, a butcher from Rouen.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister203PP277_287-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister203PP277-287">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s court was initially too scared to report William\'s death to the King. When he was finally told, he collapsed with grief.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The disaster left Henry with no legitimate son, his various nephews now the closest male heirs.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> Henry announced he would take a new wife, <a href="/wiki/Adeliza_of_Louvain" title="Adeliza of Louvain">Adeliza of Louvain</a>, opening up the prospect of a new royal son, and the two were married at <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a> in January 1121.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;nb 27&#93;</a></sup> Henry appears to have chosen her because she was attractive and came from a prestigious noble line. Adela seems to have been fond of Henry and joined him in his travels, probably to maximise the chances of her conceiving a child.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> The <i>White Ship</i> disaster initiated fresh conflict in Wales, where the drowning of Richard, Earl of Chester, encouraged a rebellion led by <a href="/wiki/Maredudd_ap_Bleddyn" title="Maredudd ap Bleddyn">Maredudd ap Bleddyn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P282_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P282-296">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> Henry intervened in North Wales that summer with an army and, although the King was hit by a Welsh arrow, the campaign reaffirmed royal power across the region.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P282_296-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P282-296">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With William dead, Henry\'s alliance with Anjou&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 which had been based on his son marrying Fulk\'s daughter&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 began to disintegrate.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P290_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P290-297">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> Fulk returned from the Levant and demanded that Henry return Matilda and her dowry, a range of estates and fortifications in Maine.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P290_297-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P290-297">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> Matilda left for Anjou, but Henry argued that the dowry had in fact originally belonged to him before it came into the possession of Fulk, and so declined to hand the estates back to Anjou.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> Fulk married his daughter <a href="/wiki/Sibylla_of_Anjou" title="Sibylla of Anjou">Sibylla</a> to William Clito, and granted them Maine.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P292_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P292-299">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> Once again, conflict broke out, as Amaury de Montfort allied himself with Fulk and led a revolt along the Norman-Anjou border in 1123.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P292_299-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P292-299">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> Amaury was joined by several other Norman barons, headed by <a href="/wiki/Waleran_de_Beaumont,_1st_Earl_of_Worcester" title="Waleran de Beaumont, 1st Earl of Worcester">Waleran de Beaumont</a>, one of the sons of Henry\'s old ally, Robert of Meulan.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;nb 28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry dispatched Robert of Gloucester and Ranulf le Meschin to Normandy and then intervened himself in late 1123.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> Henry began the process of besieging the rebel castles, before wintering in the Duchy.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> In the spring of 1124, campaigning began again. In the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bourgth%C3%A9roulde" title="Battle of Bourgth\xc3\xa9roulde">battle of Bourgth\xc3\xa9roulde</a>, Odo Borleng, castellan of <a href="/wiki/Bernay,_Eure" title="Bernay, Eure">Bernay</a>, led the king\xe2\x80\x99s army and received intelligence that the rebels were departing from the rebel base in <a href="/wiki/Beaumont-le-Roger" title="Beaumont-le-Roger">Beaumont-le-Roger</a> allowing him to ambush them as they traversed through the <a href="/wiki/Pont_de_Brotonne" title="Pont de Brotonne">Brotonne</a> forest. Waleran charged the royal forces, but his knights were cut down by Odo\'s archers and the rebels were quickly overwhelmed.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P300_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P300-305">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> Waleran was captured, but Amaury escaped.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P300_305-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P300-305">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> Henry mopped up the remainder of the rebellion, blinding some of the rebel leaders&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 considered, at the time, a more merciful punishment than execution&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 and recovering the last rebel castles.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> Henry paid Pope Callixtus a large amount of money, in exchange for the Papacy annulling the marriage of William Clito and Sibylla on the grounds of <a href="/wiki/Consanguinity" title="Consanguinity">consanguinity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;nb 29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Planning_the_succession.2C_1124.E2.80.9334"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Planning_the_succession,_1124\xe2\x80\x9334">Planning the succession, 1124\xe2\x80\x9334</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Planning the succession, 1124\xe2\x80\x9334">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Henry and his new wife did not conceive any children, generating prurient speculation as to the possible explanation, and the future of the dynasty appeared at risk.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;nb 30&#93;</a></sup> Henry may have begun to look among his nephews for a possible heir. He may have considered <a href="/wiki/Stephen,_King_of_England" title="Stephen, King of England">Stephen of Blois</a> as a possible option and, perhaps in preparation for this, he arranged a beneficial marriage for Stephen to a wealthy heiress, <a href="/wiki/Matilda_of_Boulogne" title="Matilda of Boulogne">Matilda</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> Theobald of Blois, his close ally, may have also felt that he was in favour with Henry.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> William Clito, who was King Louis\'s preferred choice, remained opposed to Henry and was therefore unsuitable.<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> Henry may have also considered his own illegitimate son, Robert of Gloucester, as a possible candidate, but English tradition and custom would have looked unfavourably on this.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry\'s plans shifted when the Empress Matilda\'s husband, the Emperor Henry, died in 1125.<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup> King Henry recalled his daughter to England the next year and declared that, should he die without a male heir, she was to be his rightful successor.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P309_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P309-318">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> The Anglo-Norman barons were gathered together at Westminster at Christmas 1126, where they swore to recognise Matilda and any future legitimate heir she might have.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollister2003P309_318-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P309-318">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;nb 31&#93;</a></sup> Putting forward a woman as a potential heir in this way was unusual: opposition to Matilda continued to exist within the English court, and Louis was vehemently opposed to her candidacy.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fresh conflict broke out in 1127, when <a href="/wiki/Charles_I,_Count_of_Flanders" title="Charles I, Count of Flanders">Charles</a>, the childless <a href="/wiki/Count_of_Flanders" title="Count of Flanders">Count of Flanders</a>, was murdered, creating a local succession crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> Backed by King Louis, William Clito was chosen by the Flemings to become their new ruler.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> This development potentially threatened Normandy, and Henry began to finance a <a href="/wiki/Proxy_war" title="Proxy war">proxy war</a> in Flanders, promoting the claims of William\'s Flemish rivals.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> In an effort to disrupt the French alliance with William, Henry mounted an attack into France in 1128, forcing Louis to cut his aid to William.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> William died unexpectedly in July, removing the last major challenger to Henry\'s rule and bringing the war in Flanders to a halt.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> Without William, the baronial opposition in Normandy lacked a leader. A fresh peace was made with France, and the King was finally able to release the remaining prisoners from the revolt of 1123, including Waleran of Meulan, who was rehabilitated into the royal court.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, Henry rebuilt his alliance with Fulk of Anjou, this time by marrying Matilda to Fulk\'s eldest son, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Plantagenet,_Count_of_Anjou" title="Geoffrey Plantagenet, Count of Anjou">Geoffrey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> The pair were betrothed in 1127 and married the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> It is unknown whether Henry intended Geoffrey to have any future claim on England or Normandy, and he was probably keeping his son-in-law\'s status deliberately uncertain. Similarly, although Matilda was granted a number of Normandy castles as part of her dowry, it was not specified when the couple would actually take possession of them.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> Fulk left Anjou for Jerusalem in 1129, declaring Geoffrey the Count of Anjou and Maine.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> The marriage proved difficult, as the couple did not particularly like each other and the disputed castles proved a point of contention, resulting in Matilda returning to Normandy later that year.<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> Henry appears to have blamed Geoffrey for the separation, but in 1131 the couple were reconciled.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> Much to the pleasure and relief of Henry, Matilda then gave birth to a sequence of two sons, <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey,_Count_of_Nantes" title="Geoffrey, Count of Nantes">Geoffrey</a>, in 1133 and 1134.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_legacy">Death and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Death and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Death.2C_1135"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Death,_1135">Death, 1135</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Death, 1135">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_I_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f6v_(detail).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Picture of Henry" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Henry_I_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f6v_%28detail%29.jpg/220px-Henry_I_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f6v_%28detail%29.jpg" width="220" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Henry_I_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f6v_%28detail%29.jpg/330px-Henry_I_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f6v_%28detail%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Henry_I_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f6v_%28detail%29.jpg/440px-Henry_I_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f6v_%28detail%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="521" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_I_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f6v_(detail).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early 14th-century depiction of Henry mourning the death of his son</div></div></div>\n<p>Relations between Henry, Matilda, and Geoffrey became increasingly strained during the King\'s final years. Matilda and Geoffrey suspected that they lacked genuine support in England. In 1135 they urged Henry to hand over the royal castles in Normandy to Matilda whilst he was still alive, and insisted that the Norman nobility swear immediate allegiance to her, thereby giving the couple a more powerful position after Henry\'s death.<sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> Henry angrily declined to do so, probably out of concern that Geoffrey would try to seize power in Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> A fresh rebellion broke out amongst the barons in southern Normandy, led by <a href="/wiki/William_III,_Count_of_Ponthieu" title="William III, Count of Ponthieu">William</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Count_of_Ponthieu" title="Count of Ponthieu">Count of Ponthieu</a>, whereupon Geoffrey and Matilda intervened in support of the rebels.<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry campaigned throughout the autumn, strengthening the southern frontier, and then travelled to <a href="/wiki/Lyons-la-For%C3%AAt" title="Lyons-la-For\xc3\xaat">Lyons-la-For\xc3\xaat</a> in November to enjoy some hunting, still apparently healthy.<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> There Henry fell ill&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 according to the chronicler <a href="/wiki/Henry_of_Huntingdon" title="Henry of Huntingdon">Henry of Huntingdon</a>, he ate too many ("a surfeit of") <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lampreys</a> against his physician\'s advice&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 and his condition worsened over the course of a week.<sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> Once the condition appeared terminal, Henry gave confession and summoned Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Hugh_of_Amiens" title="Hugh of Amiens">Hugh of Amiens</a>, who was joined by Robert of Gloucester and other members of the court.<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> In accordance with custom, preparations were made to settle Henry\'s outstanding debts and to revoke outstanding sentences of forfeiture.<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup> The King died on 1 December 1135, and his corpse was taken to Rouen accompanied by the barons, where it was embalmed; his entrails were buried locally at the <a href="/wiki/Priory" title="Priory">priory</a> of Notre-Dame du Pr\xc3\xa9, and the preserved body was taken on to England, where it was interred at Reading Abbey.<sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite Henry\'s efforts, the succession was disputed. When news began to spread of the King\'s death, Geoffrey and Matilda were in Anjou supporting the rebels in their campaign against the royal army, which included a number of Matilda\'s supporters such as Robert of Gloucester.<sup id="cite_ref-BarlowP162_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BarlowP162-18">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Many of these barons had taken an oath to stay in Normandy until the late king was properly buried, which prevented them from returning to England.<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> The Norman nobility discussed declaring Theobald of Blois king.<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> Theobald\'s younger brother, Stephen of Blois, quickly crossed from Boulogne to England, however, accompanied by his military household.<sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> With the help of his brother, <a href="/wiki/Henry_of_Blois" title="Henry of Blois">Henry of Blois</a>, he seized power in England and was crowned king on 22 December.<sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> The Empress Matilda did not give up her claim to England and Normandy, leading to the prolonged civil war known as <a href="/wiki/The_Anarchy" title="The Anarchy">the Anarchy</a> between 1135 and 1153.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Llyfr_Coch_Hergest_240-241.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of chronicle" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Llyfr_Coch_Hergest_240-241.JPG/220px-Llyfr_Coch_Hergest_240-241.JPG" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Llyfr_Coch_Hergest_240-241.JPG/330px-Llyfr_Coch_Hergest_240-241.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Llyfr_Coch_Hergest_240-241.JPG/440px-Llyfr_Coch_Hergest_240-241.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="890" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Llyfr_Coch_Hergest_240-241.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Part of the Welsh <i><a href="/wiki/Brut_y_Tywysogion" title="Brut y Tywysogion">Brut</a></i>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Chronicler" class="mw-redirect" title="Chronicler">chronicler</a> sources for Henry\'s reign</div></div></div>\n<p>Historians have drawn on a range of sources on Henry, including the accounts of chroniclers; other documentary evidence, including early <a href="/wiki/Pipe_roll" class="mw-redirect" title="Pipe roll">pipe rolls</a>; and surviving buildings and architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> The three main chroniclers to describe the events of Henry\'s life were William of Malmesbury, Orderic Vitalis, and Henry of Huntingdon, but each incorporated extensive social and moral commentary into their accounts and borrowed a range of literary devices and stereotypical events from other popular works.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> Other chroniclers include <a href="/wiki/Eadmer" title="Eadmer">Eadmer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_the_Chanter" title="Hugh the Chanter">Hugh the Chanter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abbot_Suger" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbot Suger">Abbot Suger</a>, and the authors of the Welsh <i><a href="/wiki/Brut_y_Tywysogion" title="Brut y Tywysogion">Brut</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup> Not all royal documents from the period have survived, but there are a number of royal acts, charters, writs, and letters, along with some early financial records.<sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> Some of these have since been discovered to be forgeries, and others had been subsequently amended or tampered with.<sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Late medieval historians seized on the accounts of selected chroniclers regarding Henry\'s education and gave him the title of Henry "Beauclerc", a theme echoed in the analysis of <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian</a> historians such as <a href="/wiki/Francis_Palgrave" title="Francis Palgrave">Francis Palgrave</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_William_Carless_Davis" title="Henry William Carless Davis">Henry Davis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-353" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-353">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> The historian Charles David dismissed this argument in 1929, showing the more extreme claims for Henry\'s education to be without foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> Modern histories of Henry commenced with <a href="/wiki/R._W._Southern" title="R. W. Southern">Richard Southern</a>\'s work in the early 1960s, followed by extensive research during the rest of the 20th century into a wide number of themes from his reign in England, and a much more limited number of studies of his rule in Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> Only two major, modern biographies of Henry have been produced, Warren Hollister\'s posthumous volume in 2001, and Judith Green\'s 2006 work.<sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Interpretation of Henry\'s personality by historians has altered over time. Earlier historians such as <a href="/wiki/Austin_Lane_Poole" title="Austin Lane Poole">Austin Poole</a> and Richard Southern considered Henry as a cruel, draconian ruler.<sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> More recent historians, such as Hollister and Green, view his implementation of justice much more sympathetically, particularly when set against the standards of the day, but even Green has noted that Henry was "in many respects highly unpleasant", and Alan Cooper has observed that many contemporary chroniclers were probably too scared of the King to voice much criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> Historians have also debated the extent to which Henry\'s administrative reforms genuinely constituted an introduction of what Hollister and John Baldwin have termed systematic, "administrative kingship", or whether his outlook remained fundamentally traditional.<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry\'s burial at Reading Abbey is marked by a local cross, but Reading Abbey was slowly demolished during the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">Dissolution of the Monasteries</a> in the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Duffy2003P52_360-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy2003P52-360">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> The exact location is uncertain, but the most likely location of the tomb itself is now in a built-up area of central Reading, on the site of the former abbey choir.<sup id="cite_ref-Duffy2003P52_360-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy2003P52-360">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> A plan to locate his remains was announced in March 2015, with support from <a href="/wiki/English_Heritage" title="English Heritage">English Heritage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philippa_Langley" title="Philippa Langley">Philippa Langley</a>, who aided with the successful <a href="/wiki/Exhumation_of_Richard_III_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Exhumation of Richard III of England">exhumation of Richard III</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_and_children">Family and children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Family and children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legitimate">Legitimate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span href="#cite_note-Hollister2003P130-98">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Henry possibly had a short-lived son, Richard, from his first marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-Green_2009_75_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green_2009_75-111">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Henry and his second wife, Adeliza, had no children.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Illegitimate">Illegitimate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Illegitimate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Henry had a number of illegitimate children by various mistresses.<sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">&#91;nb 32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sons">Sons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Henry_I_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Sons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Robert,_1st_Earl_of_Gloucester" title="Robert, 1st Earl of Gloucester">Robert of Gloucester</a>, born in the 1090s.<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Richard_of_Lincoln,_Norman_Prince" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard of Lincoln, Norman Prince">Richard</a>, born to Ansfride, brought up by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bloet" title="Robert Bloet">Robert Bloet</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Lincoln" title="Bishop of Lincoln">Bishop of Lincoln</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Reginald_de_Dunstanville,_1st_Earl_of_Cornwall" title="Reginald de Dunstanville, 1st Earl of Cornwall">Reginald de Dunstanville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Cornwall" title="Earl of Cornwall">Earl of Cornwall</a>, born in the 1110s or early 1120s, possibly to Sibyl Corbet.<sup id="cite_ref-366" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-366">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_FitzEdith,_Lord_Okehampton" title="Robert FitzEdith, Lord Okehampton">Robert the King\'s son</a>, born to Ede, daughter of Forne.<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson2003Green2009P322_367-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson2003Green2009P322-367">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gilbert_FitzRoy" title="Gilbert FitzRoy">Gilbert FitzRoy</a>, possibly born to an unnamed sister or daughter of Walter of Gand.<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson2003P146_368-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson2003P146-368">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>William de Tracy, possibly born in the 1090s.<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson2003P146_368-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson2003P146-368">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Henry_FitzRoy_(d._1158)" title="Henry FitzRoy (d. 1158)">Henry the King\'s son</a>, possibly born to <a href="/wiki/Nest_ferch_Rhys" title="Nest ferch Rhys">Nest ferch Rhys</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson2003Green2009P322_367-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson2003Green2009P322-367">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">&#91;nb 33&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="#cite_note-Thompson2003Green2009P322-367">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>William, the brother of Sybilla de Normandy, probably the brother of Reginald de Dunstanville.<sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Daughters">Daughters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; padding:0 0.2em;background-color: #bfc;">9. <a href="/wiki/Judith_of_Brittany" title="Judith of Brittany">Judith of Brittany</a></td><td rowspan="2" colspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="7"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="6" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="3" class="ahnentafel-b"> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; padding:0 0.2em;background-color: #9fe;">19. <a href="/wiki/Ermengarde_of_Anjou_(952%E2%80%93992)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ermengarde of Anjou (952\xe2\x80\x93992)">Ermengarde of Anjou</a></td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; padding:0 0.2em;background-color: #fb9;">2. <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of England">William I of England</a></td><td rowspan="2" colspan="11"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="31" class="ahnentafel-t"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="30" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="15" class="ahnentafel-b"> </td><td rowspan="14" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="7"> </td><td rowspan="6" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; padding:0 0.2em;background-color: #bfc;">10. <a href="/wiki/Fulbert_of_Falaise" title="Fulbert of Falaise">Fulbert of Falaise</a></td><td rowspan="2" colspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="7" class="ahnentafel-t"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="6" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; padding:0 0.2em;background-color: #ffc;">5. <a href="/wiki/Herleva" title="Herleva">Herleva</a></td><td rowspan="2" colspan="7"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="15"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="14" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="7"> </td><td rowspan="6" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; padding:0 0.2em;background-color: #fcc;">1. <b>Henry I of England</b></td><td rowspan="2" colspan="15"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="60" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="31" class="ahnentafel-b"> </td><td rowspan="30" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="15"> </td><td rowspan="14" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; padding:0 0.2em;background-color: #9fe;">24. <a href="/wiki/Arnulf_II,_Count_of_Flanders" title="Arnulf II, Count of Flanders">Arnulf II, Count of Flanders</a></td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="3" class="ahnentafel-t"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="2" colspan="4" style="border:1px solid black;height:0.5em; padding:0 0.2em;background-color: #bfc;">12. <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_IV,_Count_of_Flanders" title="Baldwin IV, Count of Flanders">Baldwin IV, Count of Flanders</a></td><td rowspan="2" colspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="7" class="ahnentafel-t"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="6" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="3" class="ahnentafel-t"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="14" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="7" class="ahnentafel-b"> </td><td rowspan="6" colspan="2"> </td><td rowspan="3"> </td><td rowspan="2" href="/wiki/Ogive_of_Luxembourg" title="Ogive of Luxembourg">Ogive of Luxembourg</a></td><td rowspan="2" colspan="3"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="7"> </td></tr><tr><td> </td><td rowspan="6"
